   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d105313657" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Micro-sized bismuth (Bi) is recognized for its high volumetric capacity and suitable working potential, making it a promising anode candidate for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, its substantial volume changes and slow reaction kinetics during cycling detrimentally affects the SIB performance. Theoretical prediction uncovers a previously unexplored favorable attribute that bonding between nitrogen within a carbon coating and Bi atoms facilitates Na<sup>+</sup> ingress into the Bi bulk, significantly enhancing Bi-Na alloying reactions, mitigating volume expansion, and preventing Na-dendrite formation. Experimentally, the study innovatively engineers a flower-like micro-sized Bi encapsulated within an elastic, nitrogen-doped carbon framework (FBi@NC) working as an efficient anode for SIBs. This design enables FBi@NC anode achieving a high tap density of 2.86&nbsp;g cm<sup>−3</sup> and delivering a remarkable volumetric capacity of 1100 mAh cm<sup>−3</sup> at 1&nbsp;mA cm<sup>−</sup><sup>2</sup>. It also exhibits exceptional rate capability (368.2&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and super durable cyclability (10&nbsp;000 cycles with 318.8&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, retaining 82% capacity). Full cells with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathodes demonstrate superior rate and cycling performances. Crucially, this study elucidates the underlying Na<sup>+</sup>-storage mechanisms and the contributory factors to performance enhancement, providing vital insights for the development of high-energy and stable SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400132-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400132-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Driven by the escalating demands for large-scale stationary energy storage systems, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as a formidable complement to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) due to their advantages of affordability and abundant resources.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0001_R_d105313648e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a>-<a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0004_R_d105313648e507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Energy Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, the insertion of large Na ions into the graphite anodes typical in commercial LIBs is thermodynamically unfavorable, positioning the anode as a key obstacle in achieving high-performance SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0005_R_d105313648e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci.">5</a>, <a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0006_R_d105313648e512" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Energy Environ. Sci.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, many SIB anodes are hindered by subpar reversible capacity, rate capability, and cyclability due to substantial volume changes and slow Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics during the continuous sodiation/desodiation cycles, significantly impeding SIB advancement.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0007_R_d105313648e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0008_R_d105313648e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, there is an imperative need to explore suitable anode materials capable of stable and reversible Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage for enhanced SIB performance. Amongst various anode candidates, bismuth (Bi), as a typical alloy-based anode material with its distinctive layered structure characterized by large interlamellar spacing (d(003) = 3.95 Å) and environmentally friendly properties, is particularly promising.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0009_R_d105313648e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nat. Chem.">9</a>-<a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0012_R_d105313648e527" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It offers a substantial volumetric capacity (3750&nbsp;mA h cm<sup>−3</sup>, fivefold that of carbonaceous anodes) and moderate redox potentials (≈ 0.55&nbsp;V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0013_R_d105313648e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Storage Mater.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite these advantages, Bi anodes are susceptible to giant volume variations, resulting in structural degradation and performance decay during repeated sodiation/desodiation processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0014_R_d105313648e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Cell Rep. Phys. Sci.">14</a>, <a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0015_R_d105313648e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the inherent semi-metal conductivity of Bi anodes poses a limitation on Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and electron transport.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0016_R_d105313648e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci.">16</a>, <a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0017_R_d105313648e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Mater.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In recent years, extensive research has been directed toward overcoming these drawbacks, primarily through reducing Bi particles to the nanoscale, integrating nanostructured Bi with various functional phases, optimizing the morphological attributes of nanostructured Bi, and innovating binder technologies.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0017_R_d105313648e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Mater.">17</a>-<a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0022_R_d105313648e557" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Sci.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These approaches have led to marked improvements in cycle stability and rate performance of Bi anodes. Despite these advancements, nanostructuring of Bi anodes, barring a few exceptions, has introduced several new critical challenges. These include reduced volumetric capacity, low mass loading, particle aggregation, escalated production costs and complexity, and reduced Coulombic efficiency, largely due to the inherent challenges associated with nanomaterials such as low tap density, high surface area, and intricate production processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0023_R_d105313648e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Commun.">23</a>-<a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0025_R_d105313648e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the contrary, micron-sized Bi anodes, favored in industry due to cost-effectiveness, appear more promising for SIBs than their nanoscale counterparts.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0025_R_d105313648e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a>, <a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0026_R_d105313648e567" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> They offer advantages in mass loading, volumetric capacity and energy density, Coulombic efficiency, and practical applicability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0025_R_d105313648e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a>, <a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0026_R_d105313648e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nonetheless, these micro-sized Bi anodes being bigger are prone to even more pronounced volumetric fluctuations and higher strain during cycling. This often leads to severe mechanical fracturing, instability of the solid–electrolyte interphase (SEI) layer, and eventually rapid capacity fading.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0025_R_d105313648e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, the larger micro-sized Bi particles, due to their sluggish Na<sup>+</sup>-storage kinetics, are more susceptible to non-uniform sodiation and suboptimal Na-alloying reactions, culminating in reduced reversible capacity and rate performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0026_R_d105313648e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">26</a>, <a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0027_R_d105313648e586" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To improve the performance of micro-sized Bi anodes, the selection of suitable electrolytes is recognized as being just as critical as the engineering of electrode structures. For instance, with the right NaPF<sub>6</sub>-diglyme electrolyte, bulk Bi anodes have been shown to spontaneously reconfigure into a porous structure during cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0028_R_d105313648e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This transformation aids Na<sup>+</sup> transport and enhances structural stability, thereby leading to enhanced cycling and rate performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0029_R_d105313648e602" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0030_R_d105313648e602" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, recent studies have highlighted that the formation of harmful HF during the SEI formation process, due to the decomposition of NaPF<sub>6</sub>, can erode Bi anodes and negatively impact their performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0031_R_d105313648e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">31</a>, <a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0032_R_d105313648e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Power Sources">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, there is a pressing need to develop and implement an elastic encapsulation layer that protects micro-sized Bi from direct electrolyte exposure while accommodating volumetric changes and facilitating the Bi-Na alloying reaction. This encapsulation should also ensure efficient electron and Na<sup>+</sup> transport during cycling. And, in-depth understanding of the interaction between the encapsulation layer and the Bi, along with the mechanistic details behind the observed performance enhancements, remains a crucial research area. Moreover, crafting micrometer-scale Bi anodes that offer rapid chargeability, extended cycle life, and substantial volumetric capacity is vital yet challenging for advancing high-performance SIBs, especially under conditions of high current density and mass loading.</p>
         
         <p>In this study, guided by theoretical insights, we establish that a N-doped carbon encapsulation layer significantly enhances the performance of Bi anodes. It achieves this by promoting Bi-Na alloying reactions, improving Na<sup>+</sup>/e<sup>−</sup> transport, mitigating volume expansion, and reducing the risk of Na dendrite formation. To this end, we present the innovative design and synthesis of a flower-like micro-sized Bi@nitrogen-doped carbon composite (FBi@NC). This structure consists of flower-like Bi microspheres enveloped within a resilient and conductive N-doped carbon framework, achieved through the thermal reduction of polyvinylpyrrolidone (PVP)-coated urchin-like micro-sized Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> in an H<sub>2</sub>/Ar atmosphere. This design ensures the structural integrity and electrical connectivity of the electrodes, as well as the electrochemical activity of the Bi microparticles, even amidst the substantial volume changes experienced by the micro-sized Bi anodes during cycling. The SEI is expected to predominantly form on the N-doped carbon framework, shielding the Bi surface from direct electrolyte contact. Thanks to its structural advantages, the resulting FBi@NC anode, with a high tap density of 2.86&nbsp;g cm<sup>−</sup><sup>3</sup>, demonstrates exceptional Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance characterized by outstanding rate capability (368.2&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>, 95.4% capacity retention with a 300-fold increase in current density), extended durability (10&nbsp;000 cycles, 318.8&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> with 82.1% capacity retention), and a large volumetric capacity of 1106.2 mAh cm<sup>−3</sup>, surpassing many previously reported Bi-based anodes in SIBs. Remarkably, even at high current densities of 10 and 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, it maintains reversible capacities of 328.8 and 246.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, with capacity retentions of 82.8% and 81.2%, respectively. Furthermore, it achieves a stable reversible capacity of 2.40&nbsp;mA h cm<sup>−2</sup> (296.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) with a 97% capacity retention over 1000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, even under a high mass loading of 8.07&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. When paired with a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode, the full cell exhibits superior rate performance (258.9&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 15 A g<sup>−1</sup>, based on anode active mass) and maintains over 94% capacity retention across 350 cycles at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Additionally, the underlying Na<sup>+</sup>-storage mechanisms and factors contributing to the performance enhancement are thoroughly elucidated using a combination of in situ techniques such as transmission electron microscopy (TEM), scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and X-ray diffraction (XRD), alongside comprehensive electrochemical tests and theoretical simulations, further supported by ex situ microscopic and spectroscopic characterizations.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400132-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400132-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>To obtain a deeper understanding of the N-type carbon layer imparting its attractive properties to Bi from the electronic and atomic scale, we systematically investigate the thermodynamic properties and kinetic processes of the interaction between Na<sup>+</sup> ions and Bi, Bi@C, as well as Bi@NC using density functional theory (DFT) calculations and ab initio molecular dynamics (AIMD) simulations. Three possible crystal surfaces of Bi, namely (001), (012), and (101) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), are considered in this study, with the surface indices being defined concerning the unit cell. According to the calculated surface energy for each surface (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), the (001) surface exhibits the lowest surface energy, indicating the highest probability of the (001) crystal surface. In contrast, the (101) surface contains numerous Bi atoms with dangling bonds, resulting in a higher surface energy and making it difficult for the (101) as the Bi surface. To best match the actual structure, we focus on three cases involving the (001) surface of Bi as the surface, namely, Bi(001) without carbon coating, Bi(001)@C with carbon coating, and Bi(001)@NC with carbon coating containing pyrrolic N defects.</p>
         
         <p>Based on the partial density of states (PDOS) and charge density near the Fermi level (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0001">1a</a>), Bi exhibits metallic conductivity primarily contributed by the Bi-6p orbitals. After carbon coating, the C-2p orbitals contribute to the Fermi level, further increasing the number of conducting electrons in the material. N doping introduces more conducting electrons from the C-2p orbitals, indicating that the N-doped carbon layer can enhance the electrical conductivity. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0001">1b</a> shows the adsorption energy (<i>E</i><sub>ad</sub>) of Na<sup>+</sup> on Bi(001), Bi(001)@C, and Bi(001)@NC surfaces. It can be observed that Na<sup>+</sup> exhibits lower adsorption energy on the Bi(001) surface after a certain number of Na<sup>+</sup> adsorptions, but it tends to form ordered Na crystallites, indicating the propensity of the formation of Na dendrite on the Bi(001) surface. With the carbon coating, the <i>E</i><sub>ad</sub> for Na<sup>+</sup> on Bi(001)@C significantly increases, presenting a non-crystalline structure. Interestingly, N doping allows the carbon coating layer to exhibit strong Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption capability while maintaining a non-crystalline structure, effectively speeding up Na<sup>+</sup> transport and preventing the formation of Na dendrites. When Na<sup>+</sup> attempts to enter the carbon coating layer, the pyrrolic N defect significantly reduces the energy barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> to penetrate the carbon layer (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0001">1c</a>), providing a transport channel for the reaction of Na with Bi.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400132-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/226659c3-5d3b-42cb-9ebf-d5e8e360d3b0/aenm202400132-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/226659c3-5d3b-42cb-9ebf-d5e8e360d3b0/aenm202400132-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4518e225-93f6-4324-a2ff-bc1117e75fdc/aenm202400132-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/226659c3-5d3b-42cb-9ebf-d5e8e360d3b0/aenm202400132-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400132-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400132" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Partial density of states (PDOS) and the charge densities near the Fermi energy level of Bi, Bi@C and Bi@NC. The density of states near the Fermi energy level is within the energy range of −0.1 to 0&nbsp;eV. b) Adsorption energy (E<sub>ad</sub>) of Na on Bi(001), Bi(001)@C, and Bi(001)@NC. c) Energy barriers for Na crossing the C layer from the Bi(001)@C surface (orange) and Bi(001)@NC surface (green). d) Calculated 3D charge density difference (CDD) of Bi(001), Bi(001)@C, and Bi(001)@NC, where charge accumulation is marked in yellow, and charge depletion is marked in blue. e) Snapshots of Molecular dynamics (MD) simulations of Na diffusion on the Bi(001), Bi(001)@C, and Bi(001)@NC and Comparison of Na diffusion coefficient (D). f) Formation energy of Na with Bi(001), Bi(001)@C, and Bi(001)@NC. Na crystals, Bi(001), Bi(001)@C, and Bi(001)@NC are used here as a reference. g) The lattice parameter changes of Bi and Bi@NC and the side views of the atomic structure (on the right graph).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The charge density difference (CDD) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0001">1d</a>) indicates that the carbon coating in Bi(001)@C and Bi(001)@NC does not experience charge transfer with Na and Bi atoms, suggesting their van der Waals interactions. Instead, it promotes the formation of the Na-Bi compound, as evident from the significant charge accumulation between Na and Bi. Through MD simulations, we reveal the diffusion and reaction processes of Na<sup>+</sup> on Bi(001), Bi(001)@C, and Bi(001)@NC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0001">1e</a>). It can be seen that, although Na<sup>+</sup> has the highest diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i>) on the Bi(001) surface within a simulation time of 10&nbsp;ps, Na arranges regularly on the surface, forming Na dendrites, and does not tend to undergo an alloying reaction. In contrast, Na on the Bi/C interface of Bi(001)@C and Bi(001)@NC can effectively alloy with Bi, suppressing the diffusion of Na and resulting in a lower <i>D</i>. The calculated formation energy (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0001">1f</a>) shows that, with crystalline Na as the reference state, carbon coating promotes the Bi-Na alloying reaction, leading to a significant reduction in the formation energy. This promoting effect is even stronger in Bi(001)@NC, as reflected by the further decreased formation energy. For the (012) surface with similar energy, Na has lower reactivity with Bi. Although the reactivity of Na with Bi (101) is still high, this is not common in real structures due to its higher surface energy (see Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the lattice parameter of Bi@NC increases by only 19.7%, compared to that of Bi by 37.6%, proving that the N-doped carbon layer could reduce the expansion of Bi to some extent. The theoretical calculations clearly indicate that the N-doped carbon layer afford a suite of favorable conditions for Bi anode to achieve enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> storage performance.</p>
         
         <p>Inspired by theoretical calculations, we performed the synthesis of FBi@NC and further evaluated its Na<sup>+</sup> storage capability. The preparation process of FBi@NC is schematically illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0002">2a</a>. First, polyvinylpyrrolidone coated urchin-like micro-sized Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@PVP) was synthesized by a simple solvothermal reaction (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The preferential absorption of PVP on the (010) plane of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> occurs in the growth process leading to the urchin-like morphology of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@PVP.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0033_R_d105313648e813" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Nanomater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By contrast, without the addition of PVP, broken and irregular micro-sized Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> was formed (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). X-ray diffraction (XRD) was conducted to confirm the phase information of these two precursors (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), diffraction peaks are well indexed to the standard pattern of orthorhombic Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> (JCPDS No. 75–1306). Subsequently, thermal reductions were performed on Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@PVP and bare Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, forming FBi@NC and bulk Bi microparticles (Bi MPs), respectively. The XRD patterns of FBi@NC and Bi MPs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0001">1b</a>) show sharp diffraction peaks that match well with standard pattern of Bi (JCPDS NO. 85–1329), suggesting that Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> was completely converted into Bi. SEM images reveal that the FBi@NC have an average diameter of 1.13&nbsp;µm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0002">2c,d</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). While Bi MPs are larger in size with irregular shape (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0002">2e</a>, TEM images demonstrate the complete coverage of carbon on FBi@NC, which is beneficial to mitigate the volume variation and promote the reaction kinetics of Bi during cycling. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0002">2f</a> represents the magnified detail for the boxed area in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0002">2e</a>. The selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0002">2g</a>) shows three diffraction rings indexed to (012), (110), and (202) crystal planes of Bi, respectively, in consistent with XRD results. Furthermore, the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0002">2h</a>) displays clear lattices fringes with a spacing of 0.328&nbsp;nm, corresponding to the (012) plane of Bi. In addition, the energy-dispersive spectrometer (EDS) mappings images in different magnifications (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0002">2i,j</a>) implying the homogeneous distribution of C and N, serving as a protective layer for Bi.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400132-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/592d3d77-6944-4033-a16e-e30a25bc6771/aenm202400132-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/592d3d77-6944-4033-a16e-e30a25bc6771/aenm202400132-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2e585dbc-fb9a-4a4e-ac08-3aba81dfc6b1/aenm202400132-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/592d3d77-6944-4033-a16e-e30a25bc6771/aenm202400132-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400132-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400132" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration for the preparation of FBi@NC. b) XRD pattern, c–d) SEM, e–f) TEM, g) SAED pattern, h) HRTEM, i–j) STEM and C, N, and Bi element mapping images of FBi@NC.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Raman spectroscopy was then used to examine the surface information of as-prepared samples. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information), the Raman spectra of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@PVP and Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub> are almost identical. The peak located at about 160 cm<sup>−1</sup> could be attributed to surface optical (SO) phonon modes, resulting from the defects on the surface of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>. Besides, the signal observed ≈966 cm<sup>−1</sup> originates from Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0033_R_d105313648e926" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - J. Nanomater.">33</a>, <a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0034_R_d105313648e926" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Alloys Compd.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After thermal reduction, one peak at 308 cm<sup>−1</sup> appears for FBi@NC and Bi MPs, specified as the Bi─O bond. In the case of FBi@NC, two additional broad peaks centered at 1350 and 1580 cm<sup>−1</sup>, assigned to the D and G bands of carbon, were detected. The corresponding <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value of FBi@NC is determined as 0.89, implying a high graphitic degree carbon and is thus likely to afford a higher electron conduction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0035_R_d105313648e942" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nano Lett">35</a>, <a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0036_R_d105313648e942" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Fourier transform infrared spectrum (FT-IR) of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@PVP presents two obvious characteristic peaks at 1662 and 1292 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which arise from vibration of C = O and C─N belonging to PVP (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the successful coating of PVP.</p>
         
         <p>Moreover, the content of Bi for FBi@NC, measured by thermal gravimetric analyzer (TGA) test, up to 89&nbsp;wt.% (the detailed calculation process and product analysis are exhibited in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information), guaranteeing a high capacity of prepared electrode. In addition, the N<sub>2</sub> absorption/desorption analysis was performed to determine the specific surface areas of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@PVP and FBi@NC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). Based on the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method, the specific surface areas of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@PVP and FBi@NC were calculated to be 24.42 and 20.98 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Further, the elemental compositions of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>, Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@PVP, and FBi@NC were explored by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS), as performed in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13–S15</a>, Supporting Information. In the high-resolution C 1s XPS spectra of Bi<sub>2</sub>S<sub>3</sub>@PVP and FBi@NC, peak centered at 287.2&nbsp;eV can be ascribed to C─N, further demonstrating the successful introduction of nitrogen. In the N 1s spectrum of FBi@NC, the signal of pyridinic N and pyrrolic N were found, accelerating the transport of Na<sup>+</sup> and electrons and improving the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0037_R_d105313648e1002" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">37</a>, <a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0038_R_d105313648e1002" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Energy Chem.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Electrochemical performance evaluations of the FBi@NC and Bi MPs as anodes in SIBs were conducted systematically. The cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of FBi@NC were presented in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0003">3a</a>. During initial cycle, a broad cathodic peak at ≈1.0&nbsp;V occurred, originating from the irreversible electrolyte decomposition and formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0039_R_d105313648e1015" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Chem. Eng. J.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the cathodic peaks located at 0.63 and 0.49&nbsp;V are attributed to the sodiation of Bi. For the anodic scan, two anodic peaks at 0.61 and 0.75&nbsp;V were detected, linking to the desodiation of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi to NaBi and NaBi to Bi.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0029_R_d105313648e1023" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0040_R_d105313648e1023" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Energy Environ. Sci.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0003">3b</a> displays the typical galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) profiles of the FBi@NC electrodes at a current density of 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The initial specific discharge/charge capacities are 589.7 and 433.9&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, corresponding to an initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 73.6%. The capacity loss during the initial cycle could be attributed to the formation of the SEI film and the side reactions,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0020_R_d105313648e1036" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Mater.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and can be compensated by electrolyte optimization,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0041_R_d105313648e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> anode structure design<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0014_R_d105313648e1046" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Cell Rep. Phys. Sci.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as well as pre-sodiation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0042_R_d105313648e1052" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Energy Chem.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The cycling performance of FBi@NC and Bi MPs electrodes at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> are depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0003">3c</a>. After 600 cycles, a high capacity of 378.3&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> can be maintained for FBi@NC, while only a reversible capacity of 361.1&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> was left for Bi MPs. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0003">3d</a> compares the rate performance of FBi@NC and Bi MPs from 0.1 to 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>. It is clear that the FBi@NC deliver exceptional rate property with the average capacities of 385.8, 386.3, 386.4, 385.7, 385.2, 384.4, 383.8, 382.4, 380.5, 377.1, and 368.2&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.5, 1, 3, 5, 8, 10, 15, 20, 25, and 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. With a 300-fold increase in current density, a capacity retention of 95.4% highlights the superior rate capability of FBi@NC. After the current density is reduced back to 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the reversible capacity of FBi@NC electrode recovers to 375.3&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. In contrast, only a reversible capacity of 162.5&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> was achieved for Bi MPs at 30 A g<sup>−1</sup>. As demonstrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0003">3e</a>, the GCD curves of the FBi@NC electrode share similar shape at different rate further confirm its exceptional rate performance, which is at least comparable or even better than those in many reported Bi anodes, as compared in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0003">3f</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400132-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/765e5d47-1727-4f20-a33f-fb100054a4c7/aenm202400132-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/765e5d47-1727-4f20-a33f-fb100054a4c7/aenm202400132-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5ff0f970-e0c9-46ef-a133-82370b2c0c8b/aenm202400132-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/765e5d47-1727-4f20-a33f-fb100054a4c7/aenm202400132-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400132-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400132" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of the FBi@NC electrode at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) GCD curves of the FBi@NC anode at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Cycling performance of FBi@NC and Bi MPs at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. d) Rate performances of two samples at various current densities. e) GCD curves of the FBi@NC at different current densities. f) Comparison of the rate performance of FBi@NC with those of previously reported Bi anodes for SIBs. g) Long-term cycling performance of two electrodes at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. h) Cycling performance of FBi@NC and Bi MPs at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>. i) Cycling performance of FBi@NC at 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>. j) Comparison of the volumetric and gravimetric capacities of FBi@NC with other reported anode materials. k) Areal capacity performance with high mass loadings of FBi@NC at 1 A g<sup>-1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In addition to superior rate performance, the FBi@NC electrode also delivers outstanding cycle performance with extremely high stability and ultra-long cycle life. After 10&nbsp;000 cycles at 5A g<sup>−1</sup>, a reversible capacity of 318.8&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> was maintained and the capacity retention was 82.1% (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0003">3g</a>). Furthermore, a high capacity of 328.8&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> over 8000 cycles at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> was also obtained with 82.8% capacity retention (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0003">3h</a>). Remarkably, the FBi@NC electrode exhibits stable cycling for over 5000 cycles even at a large current density of 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, delivering a high capacity of 246.8&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> and 81.2% capacity retention (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0003">3i</a>). However, the Bi MPs electrode perform a poor cycling lifetime, the assembled cells fail only after 1370 and 1080 cycles at 5 and 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>, sequentially. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information) displays the optical images of disassembled failed cells, visible pulverization and exfoliation were found for the Bi MPs electrode. Besides, while the separator exhibiting severe penetration of Na dendrites were observed, which penetrate the separator and cause the short circuit of cells. In contrast, a complete FBi@NC electrode and clean separator were observed after the disassembling of FBi@NC//Na cells, without the generation of dendrites even after 4000 cycles, in accord with theorical calculations.</p>
         
         <p>Improved anode volumetric capacity in high areal mass loading is a prerequisite for practical applications. In this case, the FBi@NC showcase a high tap density of 2.86&nbsp;g cm<sup>−3</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information), surpassing majority of formerly reported nanostructured Bi anode and other anode materials (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Consequently, the FBi@NC delivers a large volumetric capacity of 1106.2&nbsp;mA h cm<sup>−3</sup>, greatly exceeding most reported Bi-based, Sb-based, Sn-based, titanium oxide, and hard carbon anodes for SIBs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0003">3j</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). With high areal mass loading of 2.46, 3.14, and 5.67&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, reversible capacities of 333.9, 338.4, and 327.8&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> were maintained over 1000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). Even with a high mass loading of 8.07&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, a stable capacity of 296.5&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> was obtained after 1000 cycles (capacity retention of 97% with respect to the 7th cycle), equating to a high reversible areal-specific capacity of 2.40&nbsp;mA h cm<sup>−2</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0003">3k</a>). The performance of FBi@NC at high mass loading is also superior to most reported anodes for SIBs (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>To investigate the kinetic difference between the FBi@NC and Bi MPs anodes, CV measurements at different scan rates and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests were performed. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information), the O1, O2, R1, and R2 represent different redox peaks. According to the relationship between the peak current (<i>i</i>) and the sweep rate (<i>v</i>), the fitted <i>b</i>-value of log(<i>i</i>) versus log(<i>v</i>) plots of FBi@NC and Bi MPs anode indicates the reaction kinetics of the electrode. As seen in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0004">4a</a>, the fitted <i>b</i>-value for O1, O2, R1, and R2 peaks of Bi MPs is 0.55, 0.56, 0.56, and 0.58, respectively, which is close to 0.5. In comparison, the fitted <i>b</i>-value for O1, O2, R1, and R2 peaks of the FBi@NC is 0.70, 0.69, 0.70, and 0.73, respectively, which is higher than that of the Bi MPs anode. These results demonstrate that the Na<sup>+</sup> storage of both samples are dominated by diffusion-controlled reaction processes, while FBi@NC possesses faster reaction kinetics than Bi MPs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0043_R_d105313648e1232" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To further confirm this, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i>) value of FBi@NC and Bi MPs anode were investigated by GITT tests (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a>, Supporting Information). Obviously, the <i>D</i>-value of FBi@NC exceeded that of Bi MPs at most potentials, particularly during the sodiation process, indicating that FBi@NC exhibits better reaction kinetics (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0004">4b,c</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400132-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6b4292fb-1b0f-4b6c-8a36-f1648bc7267f/aenm202400132-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/6b4292fb-1b0f-4b6c-8a36-f1648bc7267f/aenm202400132-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/11799343-ebba-4089-b58d-b993d2d577de/aenm202400132-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6b4292fb-1b0f-4b6c-8a36-f1648bc7267f/aenm202400132-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400132-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400132" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Corresponding fitted <i>b</i>-value of the redox peaks of FBi@NC and Bi MPs. b,c) Calculated Na-ion diffusion coefficient of FBi@NC and Bi MPs from GITT results. d) The in situ GEIS plots, d<sub>1</sub>) The DRT curves, and d<sub>2</sub>) DRT intensity distribution graph of the FBi@NC during the first discharge/charge process. e) The in situ GEIS plots, e<sub>1</sub>) The DRT curves, and e<sub>2</sub>) DRT intensity distribution graph of the FBi@NC during the third discharge/charge process. Morphology evolution of f) FBi@NC and g) Bi MPs after different cycles observed by ex situ SEM. h) Schematic diagram of morphological evolution of FBi@NC during cycling.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Moreover, in situ galvanostatic electrochemical impedance spectra (GEIS) was carried out to unravel the interfacial resistance changes of FBi@NC electrodes in half-cell. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0004">4d,e</a> shows the Nyquist plots of cells at first cycle and third cycle. The semicircles exhibit sharp shrinkage at the initial discharge stage, and then stabilize during the subsequent charge process, implying that feasible charge carrier transfer can be maintained. In the third cycle, the total impedance remains essentially unchanged. To differentiate the specific electrochemical processes, the distribution of relaxation time (DRT) technique is utilized, which is a model-free analysis of the impedance changes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0044_R_d105313648e1286" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Power Sources">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Frequency domain-based Nyquist plots will be converted to time domain-based DRT spectra through mathematical transformations.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0045_R_d105313648e1291" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Electrochim. Acta">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Two dominant peaks appear in the DRT curves during the first cycle and third cycle (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0004">4d1,e1</a>), which are labeled as P1 and P2. The P1 peaks, which have a time constant of ≈10<sup>−5</sup> s, exhibit significant overlap, corresponding to the contact resistance associated with the electrical contact between active materials and the current collector.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0046_R_d105313648e1305" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - J. Power Sources">46</a>, <a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0047_R_d105313648e1305" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Power Sources">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The intensity of the P1 peaks greatly decreases at the beginning of discharge and also stabilizes afterward, which can be seen in the intensity distribution graphs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0004">4d2,e2</a>). The P2 peaks distributed between 10<sup>−4</sup> and 10<sup>−2</sup> s, representing the impedance of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0048_R_d105313648e1321" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Joule">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the early sodiation process, P2 peaks at 10<sup>−4</sup> to 10<sup>−3</sup> s represent an irreversible evolution, which can be attributed to the SEI formation. By observing the 2D intensity color map of the DRT curves, the P2 peaks move toward a larger relaxation time at alloying potentials (0.5–0.7&nbsp;V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>). We attribute this phenomenon to the charge transfer reactions occurring somewhere in the SEI layer during the Bi-Na alloying.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0049_R_d105313648e1333" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Sci. Adv.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When recharging to high potential, the relaxation time of P2 peaks decrease, suggesting that the resistance of SEI is associated with the ion transport through the SEI at high potential.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0050_R_d105313648e1338" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - J. Power Sources">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The same pattern also occurs in the third cycle and the exceedingly low resistance values reveal the fast ionic conductivity of SEI, also demonstrating the stability of SEI layer. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a> (Supporting Information) displays the in situ GEIS plots and DRT analysis of Bi MPs, suggesting that Bi MPs electrode also exhibit superior electrochemical kinetics at the beginning of the cycle, which is likely due to the outstanding electronic conductivity of Bi and the favorable compatibility of Bi with ether electrolyte as well as the high diffusivity of the NaBi and Na<sub>3</sub>Bi intermediate phases.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0029_R_d105313648e1349" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Mater.">29</a>, <a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0030_R_d105313648e1349" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Afterward, ex situ EIS after different cycles for FBi@NC electrode was also analyzed by DRT technique. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> (Supporting Information), the impedance is significantly reduced after the initial cycle, consistent with the results of in situ GEIS. After multiple cycles, the intensity of P1 and P2 remains stable, further demonstrating the long-term stability of the FBi@NC electrode and robustness of SEI.</p>
         
         <p>In addition, the morphological evolution of the FBi@NC and Bi MPs electrodes during cycling was inspected by ex situ SEM (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0004">4f,g</a>). After the initial cycle, the FBi@NC particles expand, with the extending of flower-shaped carbon. No cracks in the particles surface and no rupture of the carbon layer were observed. After 10 cycles, particle morphology disappeared and a reticulated nanostructure was formed. Then, at the 50th cycle, the 3D porous nanostructure was stabilized and interconnected. When 100 cycles have elapsed, the surface cracks of the electrode are further reduced, and a dense porous network structure was established. The porous structured Bi remains intact throughout multiple cycles, facilitating efficient electron and Na<sup>+</sup> transport across the entire electrode as well as accommodating volumetric changes, contributing to enhanced rate capability and long-term cycling performance. In addition, the fast charging performance of the FBi@NC electrode has been proven to arise from the high Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivities of the NaBi and Na<sub>3</sub>Bi intermediate phases.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0030_R_d105313648e1369" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In comparison, the broken of Bi MPs after the initial cycle were observed. Although, similar porous structure was formed in subsequent cycles, which is not tightly interconnected with visible cracks on the surface due to drastic volume expansion of electrode. The cross-sectional SEM images demonstrate that the thickness of FBi@NC deliver 45% volumetric expansion after 100 cycles, whereas the thickness of Bi MPs electrodes exhibited a significant thickness change of ≈70% accompanied by the generation of visible cracks (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information). Afterward, the specific details of the morphology changes of FBi@NC were observed by ex situ TEM (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a>, Supporting Information). After the initial cycle, a few holes emerged within the FBi@NC particles, which can be facilely filled with the DME electrolyte, facilitating subsequent morphological evolution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0029_R_d105313648e1381" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By the third turn, a porous structure has formed inside. The morphological evolution of electrode results from the interaction between the DME solvent and the Bi surface, which induces the displacement of Bi surface atoms, and promotes porous structure formation during cycling to reduce interfacial strain energy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0030_R_d105313648e1387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Mater.">30</a>, <a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0051_R_d105313648e1387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">51</a>, <a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0052_R_d105313648e1387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such a morphological evolution of FBi@NC is schematically depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0004">4h</a>.</p>
         
         <p>The SAED patterns and the EDS mappings images of electrode after 100 cycles (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a>, Supporting Information) illustrate that the porous nanostructure stays Bi. Na, P, F, C, and O are homogeneously distributed in the outer layer of Bi, indicating a uniform SEI film exists on the surface. Then, to clarify the reaction mechanism and phase evaluation during the initial sodiation–desodiation process, in situ XRD was utilized (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0005">5a</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0005">5b–e</a> displays the enlarged patterns of boxed region in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0005">5a</a>, the phase transformation is consistent with the redox peaks in the CV curves. The characteristic peaks of NaBi located at 25.5°, 31.6°, 36.5°, 37.4°, and 41.2° are indexed to (100), (101), (110), (002), and (111) crystal planes, respectively. And after sodiation, the characteristic peaks of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi are found at 20.9°, 32.8°, 33.7°, 37.9°, and 39.3°, corresponding to (101), (110), (103), (112), and (201) crystal planes, respectively. Particularly, the intensification of the BeO diffraction peak at 41.2° during stages II and V results from the coinciding NaBi peaks (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0005">5e</a>). Ex situ HRTEM images and Fast Fourier Transformation (FFT) patterns at different stages of the discharging/charging process further confirmed the phase evolution (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0005">5f–h</a> shows the magnified detail from Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26</a>, Supporting Information). After discharging to 0.5&nbsp;V, the distinct lattice fringes corresponding to (002) planes of NaBi can be observed. Further discharge to 0.01&nbsp;V, the image shows the characteristic lattice fringes of (2<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/9ede8e0d-8390-43c2-a689-fc5ab383ba10/aenm202400132-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16146832:media:aenm202400132:aenm202400132-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/aenm202400132-math-0001.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <mover accent="true">
                        <mn>2</mn>
                        <mo>¯</mo>
                     </mover>
                     <mn>1</mn>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${\mathrm{\bar{2}1}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>), (201), and (020) planes of Na<sub>3</sub>Bi. And when recharged to 1.8&nbsp;V, the lattice stripes of (012) of Bi reappear. Both in situ XRD and ex situ HRTEM demonstrate that the phase transition is highly reversible and the full redox reactions of Bi in SIBs are following the two-stage alloying/de-alloying mechanism (Bi ⇋ NaBi ⇋ Na<sub>3</sub>Bi), which is schematically shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0005">5i</a>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400132-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f54e9925-8246-4dcd-bc99-6e4ece9da693/aenm202400132-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/f54e9925-8246-4dcd-bc99-6e4ece9da693/aenm202400132-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/21250190-5550-49c4-8b58-68bc210f09fb/aenm202400132-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f54e9925-8246-4dcd-bc99-6e4ece9da693/aenm202400132-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400132-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400132" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Unraveling the alloying reaction mechanism during sodiation/desodiation process of FBi@NC electrodes: a) In situ XRD survey with a close-up on the 20.0–48.0° diffraction range. b–e) Magnified detail from in situ XRD patterns in diffraction angle ranges of 25.0–28.0°, 31.0–34.0°, 36.0–38.3°, and 39.0–42.0° corresponding to the marked rectangular regions, respectively. f–h) Ex situ HRTEM images after discharging to 0.5&nbsp;V, 0.01&nbsp;V, and charging to 1.8&nbsp;V, respectively. i) Schematic illustration of the phase transformation during the sodiation–desodiation process.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To gain a deeper understanding of the structure stability of FBi@NC, the sodiation process of the single particle was investigated by the in situ TEM technique.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0053_R_d105313648e1465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - eTransportation">53</a>, <a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0054_R_d105313648e1465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Energy Mater.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0006">6a–d</a> exhibits the time-resolved TEM images of FBi@NC during the first sodiation process (captured from Movie <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The two marked purple regions represent the area before sodiation and after sodiation, and the blue arrow denotes the diffusion distance of Na<sup>+</sup>. According to the SAED images (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0006">6e,f</a>), the FBi@NC particle undergoes complete sodiation after 180 s. The diffraction pattern changes from Bi in the initial state to Na<sub>3</sub>Bi. After complete sodiation, FBi@NC exhibits a rapid Na<sup>+</sup> motion velocity of 5&nbsp;nm s<sup>−1</sup> and experiences an area expansion of 180% (calculated by taking the ratio of the area observed before and after full sodiation), but without cracking (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0006">6g</a>). This expansion ratio is less than that of the theoretical volume expansion of Bi during the sodiation process (352%),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0012_R_d105313648e1495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> indicating the accommodation capability of the N-doped carbon. In situ SEM was also used to study the initial sodiation process of FBi@NC under multi-particle stacking (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0006">6h–k</a>, captured from Movie <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). As the sodiation process progressed, the creases on the surface of FBi@NC disappeared and without the generation of cracks. The total expansion of area of 159% can be observed for 3–4 particles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0006">6i</a>). The EDS spectrum of the red dotted box in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0006">6m,n</a> confirm the sodiation of FBi@NC (the atomic ratio of Na:Bi deviates from 3:1, on account of the influence of unreacted FBi@NC in boxed area). These results clearly demonstrate that the structural integrity of FBi@NC can be preserved without observable structural degradation, such as cracking or pulverization during the entire sodiation process, indicating superior structural stability.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400132-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/aeb26508-4e66-45b8-8034-e5407929a5e9/aenm202400132-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/aeb26508-4e66-45b8-8034-e5407929a5e9/aenm202400132-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8dadb6d7-15d8-48f3-b645-85941f840b7b/aenm202400132-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/aeb26508-4e66-45b8-8034-e5407929a5e9/aenm202400132-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400132-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400132" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–d) In situ TEM images of FBi@NC during the initial sodiation. e–f) SAED patterns of FBi@NC in the pristine state and after the first sodiation. g) The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion distance and sodiation area of FBi@NC during sodiation. h–k) In situ SEM images of FBi@NC during the sodiation. l) Areal expansion ratio of FBi@NC. m) The atomic percentage after sodiation (EDS scanning area is the red dotted box in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0006">6k</a>). n) EDS mapping images of Bi and Na.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The composition of SEI on the FBi@NC electrode after cycling is determined by XPS depth investigation and Time of flight secondary ion mass spectrometry (TOF-SIMS). The FBi@NC electrode was disassembled after completing 100 cycles. The high-resolution C 1s and F 1s spectra of the FBi@NC electrode are depicted in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0007">7a,b</a>. The characteristic peaks of C─C (284.8&nbsp;eV), C─O (286.2&nbsp;eV), and C─F (289.9&nbsp;eV in C 1s, 688.1&nbsp;eV in F 1s) decrease greatly with increasing etching depth, indicating organic components, including organic fluorine, predominate on the surfaces of SEI layer. As etching proceeds, the peak of C─F vanishes while the peak of Na─F becomes apparent. The existence of NaF not only increasing the strength of the SEI layer, but also providing a fast channel for the transfer of Na<sup>+</sup>, thus improving the electrochemical performance of the electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0055_R_d105313648e1549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Nat. Energy">55</a>, <a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0056_R_d105313648e1549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to the O 1s XPS spectrum in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a> (Supporting Information), Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> is predominantly dispersed in the outer layer of the SEI, while Na<sub>2</sub>O is more prevalent in the inner layer. To clearly reveal the evolution of surficial chemical states, 3D high-resolution C 1s, F 1s, and O 1s XPS spectra maps of the FBi@NC electrode at different etching depths are demonstrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0007">7c,d</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S27</a> (Supporting Information). The atomic ratios of Na, C, O, N, F, and Bi elements with depth profiling are summarized in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="aenm202400132-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d52c5d0f-fbe4-4382-af97-c0c280a1a58a/aenm202400132-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d52c5d0f-fbe4-4382-af97-c0c280a1a58a/aenm202400132-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/543cf93e-6dd3-4fbb-9ccb-c74f0c6b9a9f/aenm202400132-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d52c5d0f-fbe4-4382-af97-c0c280a1a58a/aenm202400132-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=aenm202400132-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Faenm.202400132" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a,b) The high-resolution C 1s and F 1s XPS spectra of FBi@NC electrode after Ar ion sputtering with different depths. c,d) the corresponding 3D high-resolution XPS spectra maps of C 1s and F 1s XPS spectra. e) TOF-SIMS sputter depth profile of various secondary ion species. f) The 3D view images of the sputtered volume corresponding to the depth profiles in (e). g) Schematic illustration of the SEI layers on FBi@NC. h) Schematic illustration of the NVP//FBi@NC full cell. i) Rate performance and j) the corresponding GCD curves of the full cell. k) Long-term cycling performance of full cell at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The insert shows the lighted LED bulbs driven by the cycled full cell.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In addition, the detailed ingredients of SEI were examined by TOF-SIMS. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0007">7e</a>, different ion fragments, including CH<sub>3</sub>ONa<sub>2</sub><sup>+</sup>, C<sub>2</sub>HONa<sub>2</sub><sup>+</sup>, BiO<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>, NaF<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>, NaO<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>, PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, NaCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>, PO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>, F<sup>−</sup>, and C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>−</sup>, can be detected through the use of ion beam bombardment. With the sputter time increases, the intensity of CH<sub>3</sub>ONa<sub>2</sub><sup>+</sup>, C<sub>2</sub>HONa<sub>2</sub><sup>+</sup>, C<sub>2</sub>HO<sup>−</sup>, PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>, and NaCO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>decreases, while signal of BiO<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>, NaO<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup>, PO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> increases accordingly. The analysis indicates the SEI layer is an organic-inorganic mixture, while the outer layer consists mainly of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> and organic components, and the inner layer has a higher proportion of inorganic components, corresponding to the XPS results. The 3D distribution view images of the sputtered volume correspond to the depth profiles in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0007">7f</a>. Particularly, the signal intensity of F<sup>−</sup> has not significantly changed, indicating that F is uniformly distributed in the SEI layer. The conceivable SEI structure on FBi@NC is schematically shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0007">7g</a>. Overall, the formation of an inorganic-based, F-rich SEI layer is advantageous for improving charge transfer kinetics and prolonging the cycle life of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0014_R_d105313648e1684" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Cell Rep. Phys. Sci.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To further demonstrate the potential of FBi@NC as a viable anode material in practical applications, full cells were built by paring the FBi@NC anode with a Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) cathode (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0007">7h</a>). The NVP cathode was chosen owing to its great cycling stability, fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and superior thermal stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#aenm202400132-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="aenm202400132-bib-0057_R_d105313648e1705" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Adv. Energy Mater.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S29</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a> (Supporting Information) show the structural characterizations and electrochemical performance of the NVP cathode. The typical charging/discharging profiles of the NVP//FBi@NC, NVP//Na and FBi@NC//Na cells are presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S31</a> (Supporting Information). The voltage window of the full cell was found to be 2.2–3.3&nbsp;V. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0007">7i</a>, the full cell exhibits an impressive rate performance with an average charge capacity (based on the anode active mass) of 307.6, 279.9, 263.3, 261.7, 260.6, and 258.9&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5, 1, 3, 5, 10, and 15 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. When the current density returns to 1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the capacity can be retrieved to 281.9&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>. Markedly, the corresponding GCD profiles at different rates are highly congruent, reflecting the superior rate performance of the full cell (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0007">7j</a>). Meanwhile, the GCD profiles of long-term cycling performance are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S32</a> (Supporting Information). After 350 cycles, the full cell retains a high reversible capacity of 295.9&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a capacity retention of 94.1%, still allowing the light-emitting diode (LED) bulbs to light up after cycling (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#aenm202400132-fig-0007">7k</a>). Overall, the FBi@NC full cells manifest fast charging capability and stable cycling performance, demonstrating the great potential of FBi@NC as an anode material for high energy density SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="aenm202400132-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="aenm202400132-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, our study elucidates the pivotal role of N-doped carbon frameworks in augmenting the performance of Bi anodes, as substantiated through DFT calculations and AIMD simulations. The design leverages several advantageous attributes for heightened performance: 1) The N-doped carbon layer significantly boosts electrical conductivity; 2) Pyrrolic N defects notably lower the energy barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> penetration, facilitating a conducive channel for Na<sup>+</sup> transport; 3) The synergistic interaction between N in the carbon coating and Bi atoms curtails the formation of ordered Na crystallites and actively propels the Na-Bi alloying reaction, thus deterring Na dendrite growth and accommodating volumetric changes. Guided by these theoretical predictions, we have meticulously engineered and synthesized flower-shaped Bi microspheres fully enveloped by flexible N-doped carbon frameworks, culminating in a flower-like micro-sized Bi @ N-doped carbon composite (FBi@NC). Owing to its structural superiorities, the FBi@NC anode manifests extraordinary rate capability and an impressive volumetric capacity of 1106.2&nbsp;mA h cm<sup>−3</sup>, coupled with exceptional long-term durability (318.8&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> over 10&nbsp;000 cycles with 82.1% capacity retention) and substantial areal capacity of 2.40&nbsp;mA h cm<sup>−2</sup> (296.5&nbsp;mA h g<sup>−1</sup>) with 97% capacity retention across 1000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> under high mass loading of 8.07&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. Moreover, the fully assembled NVP//FBi@NC cell also exhibits remarkable rate performance and prominent cycling stability (94% capacity retention over 350 cycles at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>), underscoring its practical applicability. This notable Na<sup>+</sup>-storage proficiency stems from a 3D nanoporous Bi anode structure that evolves during cycling to buffer volume changes and promote continuous Na<sup>+</sup> transport, with the N-doped carbon framework facilitating redox reaction kinetics, preventing Na dendrite growth and ensuring stable SEI formation, as evidenced by extensive experimental characterizations and theoretical analyses. Our findings offer profound insights into the utility of N-doped carbon encapsulation strategies for developing micro-sized alloying-type anodes with rapid chargeability and prolonged cycle life for high-performance SIBs, and are poised to propel forward the quest for high-energy-density SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
